   text-darkBlue border-b-pink autofill:text-darkBlue" value=""/></div></form></div></div></div></section></div><div class="absolute top-0 overflow-hidden w-screen transition-all ease-in-out duration-300 opacity-0 -translate-x-24 min-h-0 pointer-events-none h-full"></div></div><div style="top:NaNpx" class="transition-all ease-in-out duration-300 opacity-0 absolute -z-1 bottom-0 bg-gradient-to-b from-black to-white/0 inset-0 w-screen pointer-events-none"></div></nav></header><div class="node-blog"><div class="light_hero_module_root__dky_w container py-20 md:py-35"><div class="flex justify-between flex-col lg:flex-row lg:items-center"><p class="text-darkGrey-100 typo-heading-4  mb-20 md:mb-20 lg:mb-0 lg:text-[36px]">Zscaler Blog</p><div class="lg:flex flex justify-between flex-row items-center"><p class="text-darkGrey font-bold  mb-20 text-[12px] mr-20 md:mb-0 lg:text-[14px]">Get the latest Zscaler blog updates in your inbox</p><a class="cta_root___bSOJ light_hero_module_subscribe__cbb4s cta_btnPrimary__j_EeL cta_btnDark__U__lq" href="#subscribe-blog" aria-label="Subscribe" target="_self" click-cta="btn-cta-section" rel="noopener">Subscribe</a></div></div></div><div class=""><div class="blog_post_header_module_root__YPrt6 undefined min-h-0"><div class="tile-container relative px-20"><div class="

                        " style="font-size:1.4rem;line-height:1.8rem">April 17, 2024<!-- --> <!-- -->-<!-- --> <!-- -->19 min read</div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="container 7.093z"></path></svg></a></div><div class="sidebar_titlesWrapper__QElZv py-16"><p class="sidebar_navTopLabel__xl_5k">Contents</p><ol><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK sidebar_linkActive__wuL2e" blogs</a></li></ol></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0  hidden lg:flex" style="border-bottom:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">Copy URL</button></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0 flex lg:hidden" style="border-bottom:none;border-top:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">Copy URL</button></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden lg:block" style="margin-bottom:-0px"></div><div class="" id="introduction"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Introduction</h2><p>Beginning in March of 2024, Zscaler ThreatLabz observed a threat actor weaponizing a cluster of domains masquerading as legitimate IP scanner software sites to distribute a previously unseen backdoor. The threat actor registered multiple look-alike domains using a typosquatting technique and leveraged Google Ads to push these domains to the top of search engine results targeting specific search keywords, thereby luring victims to visit these sites.</p><p>The newly discovered backdoor uses several techniques such as multiple stages of DLL sideloading, abusing the DNS protocol for communicating with the command-and-control (C2) server, and evading memory forensics security solutions. We named this backdoor “MadMxShell” for its use of DNS MX queries for C2 communication and its very short interval between C2 requests.</p><p>In this blog, we will examine the campaign details, threat actor&#x27;s infrastructure, and a detailed technical analysis of the backdoor. We have also shared a custom Python script to decode C2 traffic for malware samples and all the Indicators of Compromise (IOCs) linked to this campaign.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="key-takeaways"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Key Takeaways</h2><ul><li>Between November 2023 and March 2024, multiple domains were registered by a threat actor spoofing legitimate IP scanners and other software typically used by IT security and network administration teams in enterprises.</li><li>The threat actor abused Google Ads to conduct a malvertising campaign in an attempt to push their malicious sites to the top of search results.</li><li>A successful infection results in the delivery of a previously unseen backdoor that we named “MadMxShell”.</li><li>The backdoor uses techniques such as multiple stages of DLL sideloading and DNS tunneling for command-and-control (C2) communication as a means to evade endpoint and network security solutions, respectively.</li><li>In addition, the backdoor uses evasive techniques like anti-dumping to prevent memory analysis and hinder forensics security solutions.</li></ul>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="background"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Background</h2><p>The selection of spoofed software by this threat actor suggests that their targets primarily consist of IT professionals, particularly those in IT security and network administration roles. This aligns with the recent trend observed where advanced persistent threat (APT) groups, such as <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2021/10/25/nobelium-targeting-delegated-administrative-privileges-to-facilitate-broader-attacks/">NOBELIUM</a>, crafted attacks targeting these teams. With their privileged access to internal systems and networks, IT security and network management teams are attractive targets for both APT groups and initial access brokers (IABs) that sell access to compromised networks. Although we have not yet attributed the attack described in this blog to a specific threat actor, it is important to highlight this emerging trend.</p><p>Threat actors have previously leveraged Google malvertising to distribute trojanized versions of a specific port scanning tool called Advanced IP Scanner, as described in reports by <a href="https://securelist.com/uncommon-infection-and-malware-propagation-methods/107640/">Kaspersky</a>, <a href="https://blogs.blackberry.com/en/2022/10/unattributed-romcom-threat-actor-spoofing-popular-apps-now-hits-ukrainian-militaries">BlackBerry</a>, and <a href="https://www.huntress.com/blog/analyzing-a-malicious-advanced-ip-scanner-google-ad-redirection">Huntress</a>.</p><p>Although the campaign discussed in this blog uses a similar distribution method, the range of spoofed software has been expanded beyond Advanced IP Scanner. Furthermore, to the best of our knowledge, the final malware delivered in this campaign has not been publicly documented before. Due to these significant differences, we assess with a high-confidence level that this campaign was conducted by a different threat actor.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="attack-chain"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Attack Chain</h2><p>The figure below illustrates the multi-stage attack chain at a high level.</p><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/attack_chain.png" alt="Figure 1: The MadMxShell end-to-end attack-chain, which starts with malvertising, followed by multiple intermediate stages of DLL sideloading, and finally DNS tunneling to the C2 server." loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p>Figure 1: The MadMxShell end-to-end attack-chain, which starts with malvertising, followed by multiple intermediate stages of DLL sideloading, and finally DNS tunneling to the C2 server.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="technical-analysis"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Technical Analysis</h2><p>In the following section, we provide a detailed analysis for each stage of the attack chain.</p><h3>Google malvertising campaign</h3><p>The modus operandi of the threat actor includes registering multiple look-alike domains spoofing popular port scanning software and pushing them to the top of Google search results by running Google Ads campaigns. This technique is widely known as <strong>malvertising</strong>.</p><p>During our investigation, we observed users being served these ads when they searched for keywords related to any of the following:</p><ul><li>Any of the legitimate port scanning and IT management software spoofed by this threat actor<ul><li>Advanced IP Scanner</li><li>Angry IP Scanner</li><li>PRTG IP Scanner by Paessler</li><li>Manage Engine</li></ul></li><li>Network admin tasks related to virtual local area networks (VLANs)</li><li>Scanning IP protocol</li></ul><p>The figure below shows details of the Google Ads campaign carried out by the threat actor in March 2024 for one of the malicious domains. The domain in question was advanced-ip-scanz[.]net, and the search keywords were:</p><ul><li>&quot;advanced ip scanner&quot;</li><li>&quot;ip address scanner&quot;</li></ul><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/google_ad_campaign_details.png" alt="Figure 2: Details of the Google Ad campaign in March 2024 for the malicious domain advanced-ip-scanz[.]net." loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p>Figure 2: Details of the Google Ads campaign in March 2024 for the malicious domain advanced-ip-scanz[.]net.</p><p>Once the user clicks on any of the attacker-controlled Google Ads, they are redirected to a look-alike site for the corresponding IP scanning software.</p><h3>Malicious sites</h3><p>The threat actor registered multiple sites masquerading as legitimate IP and port scanner software programs. One such site we observed is advansed-ip-scanner[.]net, which is a look-alike site of the legitimate Advanced IP scanner software www.advanced-ip-scanner[.]com.</p><p>The complete source code of the fraudulent website mirrors the legitimate site, with the exception of minor edits made by the threat actor to JavaScript (JS) code which redirects the user to download a malicious file when they click the download button.</p><p>The figure below shows a comparison between the altered JS code from the malicious file and the original legitimate website. The <code>createFunctionWithTimeout</code> function was modified to redirect users to download a malicious ZIP archive file from the following URL: <br/>advansed-ip-scanner[.]net/yftyudruo.php.</p><p><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/js_code_comparison.png" alt="Figure 3: JavaScript code comparison between the legitimate website’s createFunctionWithTimeout function with the malicious website." loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><br/>Figure 3: JavaScript code comparison between the legitimate website’s <code>createFunctionWithTimeout</code> function and the malicious website&#x27;s code.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="backdoor-details---binary-analysis"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Backdoor Details - Binary Analysis</h2><h3>Stage 1 injector</h3><p>The analysis in this blog is based on this ZIP archive: <code>Advanced-ip-scanner.zip (SHA256:7966ee1ae9042e7345a55aa98ddeb4f39133216438d67461c7ee39864292e015)</code>.</p><p>The ZIP archive contains two files:</p><ul><li><code>Advanced-ip-scanner.exe</code>: A renamed copy of the legitimate Microsoft EXE <code>oleview.exe</code>.</li><li><code>IVIEWERS.dll</code>: A 22 MB DLL, which contains the stage two payload. This DLL is padded with an unused overlay of 10 MB which prevents scanning by security products that limit the size of analyzed files.</li></ul><p>When <code>Advanced-ip-scanner.exe</code> is run, it sideloads <code>IVIEWERS.dll</code> which executes a series of heavily obfuscated shellcodes extracted from various locations within the <code>.rsrc</code> section of the DLL. The final shellcode extracts and decodes an executable file with the XOR key <code>5dsadas435235bgdsgdfbvb3253453425345gfdsgfdgdf</code> from resource <code>AT21</code> of the DLL and injects it into a new <code>Advanced-ip-scanner.exe</code> process via process hollowing.</p><h3>Stage 2 dropper</h3><p>The injected EXE file contains the next stage payloads in resource ID <code>202</code>, encoded with a hardcoded 8-byte XOR key <code>F2 09 CD 2D 85 CD 1D A3</code> and compressed with zlib. Each encoded byte in this resource is padded with seven null bytes, resulting in a 10MB file, likely as another anti-scanning technique. This is shown in the figure below.</p><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/stage_3_payload.png" alt="Figure 4: The encoded and compressed stage 3 payload in the resource." loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p>Figure 4: The encoded and compressed stage 3 payload in the resource.</p><p>After decoding and decompressing the resource, two files, <code>OneDrive.exe</code> and <code>Secur32.dll</code>, are dropped into <code>%LOCALAPPDATA%\Microsoft\OneDrive\Update</code>.</p><p>The dropper deletes the stage 1 EXE with the following command before executing the dropped <code>OneDrive.exe</code> with <code>ShellExecuteExW</code>:</p><pre><code class="language-shell">cmd.exe /C for /l %x in (0,0,0) do (ping -n 3 127.0.0.1 &gt; NUL &amp; for %p in (&quot;&lt;PATH&gt;\Advanced-ip-scanner.exe&quot;) do (del /f /q %p &amp; if not exist %p exit))</code></pre><h3><br/>Stage 3 launcher</h3><p><code>OneDrive.exe</code>, a legitimate signed Microsoft EXE, is abused to sideload <code>Secur32.dll</code> which sets up persistence for <code>OneDrive.exe</code> before executing the embedded stage 4 shellcode.</p><p>Data from <code>Secur32.dll</code>’s icon resource ID <code>202</code> is XOR decoded to obtain the stage 4 shellcode. This is shown in the figure below.</p><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/stage_4_payload.png" alt="Figure 5: The icon resource with the encoded stage 4 payload." loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p>Figure 5: The icon resource with the encoded stage 4 payload.</p><p>The first 16 bytes of the resource contain an encoded key for the payload that follows it. Each of the first 8 lowercase characters (onedrive) of the current process filename is added to every second byte of the encoded key to derive the XOR key <code>F2 78 CD 9B 85 32 1D 07 33 C4 A0 21 98 A2 95 E3</code>, as shown in the figure below. This prevents the correct decoding of the next stage payload if <code>Secur32.dll</code> was not sideloaded by <code>OneDrive.exe</code>.</p><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/generating_xor_key.png" alt="Figure 6: Generating the XOR key to decode the stage 3 shellcode." loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p>Figure 6: Generating the XOR key to decode the stage 3 shellcode.</p><p>The malware then attempts to disable Windows Defender by setting <code>HKLM\SOFTWARE\Policies\Microsoft\Windows Defender\DisableAntiSpyware</code> to <code>1</code> in the registry.</p><p>It configures a scheduled task named “OneDrive Update” that executes <code>%LOCALAPPDATA%\Microsoft\OneDrive\Update\OneDrive.exe</code> when the current user logs on to Windows before redirecting to the next stage.</p><h3>Stage 4 backdoor</h3><p>The shellcode is a backdoor that allows the threat actor to collect system information, execute commands via <code>cmd.exe</code>, and perform basic file manipulation operations such as reading, writing, and deleting files.</p><p>To deter analysis and detection, the malware decodes the code of each function with an 8-byte XOR key <code>F2 09 CD 2D 85 CD 1D A3</code> (same key used in stage 2), calls the function, and then immediately re-encodes the code. In the figure below, the code excerpt on the right is decoded from the original bytes on the left. We can observe that even after decoding, it still needs to perform an additional step of decoding the <code>get_c2_domain</code> function before it can call it. The function code is re-encoded back to its original state before execution continues. This ensures that there is never a fully decoded copy of the shellcode in memory at any point of execution. Most sensitive strings and data, such as the C2 domain, lookup table for encoding/decoding C2 communications, and aforementioned XOR key, are also stored as stack strings to hinder analysis.</p><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/function_bytes.png" alt="Figure 7: Function bytes before and after decoding. Note decoded function includes calls to decode and encode other functions called within it.  " loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p>Figure 7: Function bytes before and after decoding. Note that the decoded function includes calls to decode and encode other functions called within it.  </p><p>The malware generates a 4-byte session ID with the <code>CryptGenRandom</code> API and a victim ID by concatenating the hard disk serial number, computer name, and username, and taking the first 8 bytes of its MD5 hash.</p><h4>C2 Protocol</h4><p>The malware communicates with the C2 server, litterbolo[.]com, by sending requests and receiving commands encoded within DNS MX queries and responses.</p><p>The malware supports the requests described in the table below.</p><table><tbody><tr><td><strong>Type</strong></td><td><strong>Name</strong></td><td><strong>Description</strong></td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>Heartbeat</td><td>Indicates that the malware is ready to accept the next command.</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>Registration</td><td>Sent as the first request of a session or when the C2 issues a re-registration command (type 1 command).</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>Command acknowledgement</td><td>Acknowledges the receipt of C2 commands.</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>System info command result</td><td>Contains system information collected for type 4 commands.</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>Shell command result</td><td>Contains shell output for type 5 commands.</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>File command result</td><td>Contains file and/or directory data for type 6 commands.</td></tr></tbody></table><p>Table 1: A table describing the requests supported by the malware during C2 communication. </p><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/madmxshell_communication_loop.png" alt="Figure 8: A diagram depicting the MadMxShell C2 communication loop." loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p>Figure 8: A diagram depicting the MadMxShell C2 communication loop.</p><p>For each session, the malware first sends a registration request (type 1) to the C2 server.</p><p>Once the C2 server acknowledges the registration request, the malware sends a heartbeat request (type 0) to the C2 server.</p><p>The C2 server will respond with any of the following commands from the table below.</p><table><tbody><tr><td><strong>Command Type</strong></td><td><strong>Subcommand</strong></td><td><strong>Description</strong></td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>N/A</td><td>Sends a heartbeat.</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>N/A</td><td>Re-register with C2.</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>N/A</td><td>C2 acknowledges receipt of specified packet and indicates that the malware should send the next packet (for request messages split into multiple packets). </td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>N/A</td><td><p>Collects system information, like:</p><ul><li>Computer name</li><li>User name</li><li>Ethernet IP addresses</li><li>Windows OS version</li><li>Processor name</li><li>Display card name</li><li>RAM size</li></ul></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="3">5</td><td>0</td><td>Start <code>cmd.exe</code> process.</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>Terminate <code>cmd.exe</code> process.</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>Execute command via existing <code>cmd.exe</code> process created with subcommand ID <code>0</code>.</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="4">6</td><td>0</td><td>List files and directories if path is specified, otherwise list all drives.</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>Write or append content to file.</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>Read from file.</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>Delete file or directory. Files are deleted with the <code>DeleteFileW</code> API, while directories are deleted with this command: <code>cmd.exe /c rmdir &quot;&lt;DIR_NAME&gt;&quot; /s /q</code>.</td></tr></tbody></table><p>Table 2: The commands and subcommands supported by the malware.</p><p>The malware then acknowledges the command with a command acknowledgement request (type 2) before executing the specified command.</p><p>After completing the commands for types 4, 5, and 6, the malware sends the results to the C2 server. The malware then repeats the entire process by sending a heartbeat request (type 0) to retrieve the next command. </p><h4>Data Encoding </h4><p>The malware sends requests to the C2 server by encoding the data in the subdomain(s) of the Fully Qualified Domain Name (FQDN) in a DNS MX query packet. The C2 responds similarly by encoding its commands as subdomain(s) in the corresponding DNS MX response packet.</p><p>Each byte of binary data is converted into a pair of alphanumeric characters using a custom encoding scheme involving a hardcoded 36-character lookup table. Blocks of 60 alphanumeric characters are separated by a “.” character to represent a subdomain name. Python code for decoding these subdomains into the original request and C2 messages can be found in our <a href="https://github.com/threatlabz/tools/blob/main/madmxshell/network_decoder.py">GitHub repository</a>.</p><p>Because the malware uses a maximum of 224 characters for the FQDN and the C2 domain name cannot be used to encode data, each DNS packet can only transfer up to 103 bytes. Requests and commands that exceed this size are split into multiple DNS packets and are sent sequentially after the other party has acknowledged receipt of the previous packet.</p><p>Possibly due to the limited bandwidth of the C2 protocol, this malware is configured with relatively short intervals (3 seconds) between requests. Because of this, its C2 traffic is significantly more noisy than the typical malware utilizing HTTP for C2 communication.</p><p>Commands received from the C2 (after decoding) are structured as shown in the table below.</p><table><tbody><tr><td><strong>Offset</strong></td><td><strong>Length</strong></td><td><strong>Name</strong></td><td><strong>Description</strong></td></tr><tr><td>0x0</td><td>4</td><td>Query number</td><td>Starts from <code>0</code> per session.</td></tr><tr><td>0x4</td><td>4</td><td>Checksum</td><td>Adler-32 checksum of entire message data.</td></tr><tr><td>0x8</td><td>4</td><td>Packet number</td><td>A single message may be split into multiple packets. Starts from <code>0</code> for each message.</td></tr><tr><td>0xC</td><td>4</td><td>Message length</td><td>Total length of the message. This field is only present in the first packet of a message.</td></tr><tr><td>0x10</td><td>Varies</td><td>Message data</td><td>The first byte of the message contains the command ID. The structure of subsequent bytes differs slightly for each command type.</td></tr></tbody></table><p>Table 3: The C2 message structure.</p><p>For example, the C2 server for this sample always responds with <code>33qqooggxr77mdxx88jj6600ev44yyzz9bee99wwuu.litterbolo.com</code> upon receiving a registration request. This is decoded as <code>00 00 00 00 03 00 0f 00 00 00 00 00 05 00 00 00 02 00 00 00 00</code> and represents the following message:</p><ul><li>Query number: <code>0</code></li><li>Checksum: <code>0xF0003</code></li><li>Packet number: <code>0</code></li><li>Message length: <code>5</code></li><li>Data: <code>02 00 00 00 00</code> (this is an acknowledgement from the C2 that it received packet <code>0</code>)</li></ul><p>Likewise, the requests sent to the C2 server (before encoding) are structured as shown in the table below.</p><table><tbody><tr><td><strong>Offset</strong></td><td><strong>Length</strong></td><td><strong>Name</strong></td><td><strong>Description</strong></td></tr><tr><td>0x0</td><td>4</td><td>Session ID</td><td>An ID randomly generated when the malware is started.</td></tr><tr><td>0x4</td><td>8</td><td>Victim ID</td><td>The first 8 bytes of an MD5 hash of the hard disk serial number, computer name, and user name.</td></tr><tr><td>0xc</td><td>4</td><td>Query number</td><td>Starts from <code>0</code> per session and is incremented for each DNS query sent to the C2 server.</td></tr><tr><td>0x10</td><td>4</td><td>Checksum</td><td>The Adler-32 checksum of entire message data.</td></tr><tr><td>0x14</td><td>4</td><td>Packet number</td><td>A single message may be split into multiple packets. Starts from <code>0</code> for each message.</td></tr><tr><td>0x18</td><td>4</td><td>Message length</td><td>Total length of the message. This field is only present in the first packet of a message.</td></tr><tr><td>0x1c</td><td>Varies</td><td>Message data</td><td>The first byte of the message contains the request type. Data for the specific request follows it (for example: system information for request type 4).</td></tr></tbody></table><p>Table 4: The request message structure.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="observed-commands"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Observed Commands</h2><p>During our investigation, we observed the backdoor receiving the following commands from the C2 server:</p><ul><li>Collect system information (command type 4).</li><li>Run <code>systeminfo</code> and <code>ipconfig</code> via <code>cmd.exe</code> (command type 5).</li><li>Enumerate drives and specific directories, particularly the Windows system directory and user directories (command type 6).</li></ul><p>Some of the commands were received 60 mins to 90 mins after the backdoor registered with the C2 server, which may indicate an anti-analysis technique to defeat sandboxes or actual hands-on activity by the threat actor.</p><p>Based on the capabilities of the stage 4 backdoor and the commands collected, we believe the attacker is likely interested in harvesting and exfiltrating information from infected machines. By focusing on IT teams, this threat actor can target users that have privileged access to sensitive systems (e.g., domain controllers) that can lead to a significant breach.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="infrastructure-details"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Infrastructure Details</h2><p>Our analysis started with the domain, advansed-ip-scanner[.]net, that was live at the time of analysis and was serving a payload. WHOIS information for this domain revealed the attacker&#x27;s email address used for registration to be <a href="/cdn-cgi/l/email-protection" class="__cf_email__" data-cfemail="7b0c1343434f494f434b3b1c161a121755181416">[email&#160;protected]</a>.</p><p>A quick reverse WHOIS lookup using this email address revealed 45 domains registered between November 2023 and March 2024 to spoof various network scanning and IT management software.</p><p>The complete list of domains used for malware distribution is provided in the IOCs section at the end of this blog.</p><p>These domains were hosted on servers exclusively abused by the threat actor and belonged to the ASNs below:</p><ul><li><code>AS208312 (REDBYTES, RU)</code></li><li><code>AS16276 (OVH, FR)</code></li></ul><p>The C2 domain litterbolo[.]com used a dedicated <code>nameserver</code> since the malware abused the DNS protocol for C2 communication.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="osint-research"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>OSINT Research</h2><p>Upon further Open-Source Intelligence (OSINT) research, we discovered two accounts created by the threat actor on criminal underground forums like blackhatworld[.]com and social-eng[.]ru using the email address <a href="/cdn-cgi/l/email-protection" class="__cf_email__" data-cfemail="e7908fdfdfd3d5d3dfd7a7808a868e8bc984888a">[email&#160;protected]</a>.</p><p>On the blackhatworld[.]com forum, the threat actor made two posts in a <a href="https://www.blackhatworld.com/seo/adwords-mastermind-complete-guide-to-setting-up-unlimited-adwords-threshold-accounts.1504361/">thread</a> related to someone offering methods to bypass the Google Adsense threshold. This aligns with the Google Ads abuse technique used by the threat actor to launch their own malvertising campaign.</p><p>The figures below show two posts made by the threat actor on blackhatworld[.]com expressing interest in this technique and asking to enroll in the course.</p><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/blackhat_website_posts.png" alt="Figure 9: Posts made by the threat actor showing interest in the Google Ad abuse course." loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p>Figure 9: Posts made by the threat actor showing interest in the Google Ads abuse course.</p><p>Google Ads threshold accounts and techniques for abusing them are often traded on BlackHat forums. Many times they offer a way for the threat actor to add as many credits as possible to run Google Ads campaigns. This allows the threat actors to run campaigns without actually paying until the threshold limit. A reasonably high threshold limit lets the threat actor run the ad campaign for a significant amount of time. Once the threshold has reached, they can use the same technique with a new Google Ads account to repeat the process.</p><p>Threat actors often use virtual credit cards (VCC) along with residential proxies to verify these Google Ads accounts and employ various methods to use them up to the maximum threshold.</p><p>This approach effectively enables threat actors to run long lasting malvertising campaigns with a low investment and also avoid account suspension.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="conclusion"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Conclusion</h2><p>The threat discussed in this blog demonstrates advanced tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs), displaying a keen interest in targeting users in the IT security and network administration teams. The threat actor put significant effort into remaining undetected by evading memory forensics and network security controls.</p><p>While we cannot currently attribute this activity to any known threat actor, we continue to monitor any new developments associated with this threat actor and ensure the necessary protections are in place for our customers against these threats.</p><p>We also suggest users follow security best practices and exercise caution when clicking on links appearing in Google search engine results. Users must also ensure to download software only from the official website of the developer.</p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="zscaler-coverage"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Zscaler Coverage</h2><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/zscaler_coverage_madmxshell.png" alt="Figure 10: Zscaler sandbox detection report" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/><p>Figure 10: Zscaler sandbox detection report</p><p>In addition to sandbox detections, Zscaler’s multilayered cloud security platform detects indicators related to MadMxShell at various levels with the following threat names:</p><p><a href="https://threatlibrary.zscaler.com/?threatname=madmxshell">Win32.Backdoor.MadMxShell</a></p>

</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="mitre-att-ck-framework"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>MITRE ATT&amp;CK Framework</h2><table><tbody><tr><td><strong>ID</strong></td><td><strong>Tactic</strong></td><td><strong>Description</strong></td></tr><tr><td>T1583.001</td><td>Acquire Infrastructure: Domains</td><td>The threat actor registered typosquatting domains and set up fake websites masquerading as legitimate software websites to deliver malware.</td></tr><tr><td>T1583.002</td><td>Acquire Infrastructure: DNS Server</td><td>The threat actor configured a DNS server at litterbolo[.]com for C2 communication.</td></tr><tr><td>T1583.008</td><td>Acquire Infrastructure: Malvertising</td><td>The threat actor leveraged Google malvertising, targeting search keywords related to IP and port scanners to lure users to visit malicious sites.</td></tr><tr><td>T1204.002</td><td>User Execution: Malicious File</td><td>The attack chain is started by the user when they execute the fake <code>Advanced-ip-scanner.exe</code> file.</td></tr><tr><td>T1574.002</td><td>Hijack Execution Flow: DLL Side-Loading</td><td>The threat actor leveraged two stages of DLL sideloading to execute the final payload.</td></tr><tr><td>T1055.012</td><td>Process Injection: Process Hollowing</td><td>The threat actor employs process hollowing to inject and execute the stage 2 dropper EXE file.</td></tr><tr><td>T1562.001</td><td>Impair Defenses: Disable or Modify Tools</td><td>The stage 3 launcher attempts to disable Windows Defender.</td></tr><tr><td>T1070.004</td><td>Indicator Removal: File Deletion</td><td>The stage 2 dropper runs a command via <code>cmd.exe</code> to delete the stage 1 EXE from disk.</td></tr><tr><td>T1053.005</td><td>Scheduled Task/Job: Scheduled Task </td><td>The stage 3 launcher configures for persistence by masquerading as a scheduled task. </td></tr><tr><td>T1036.004</td><td>Masquerading: Masquerade Task or Service</td><td>The scheduled task masquerades as a OneDrive update to execute <code>OneDrive.exe</code> for malware persistence.</td></tr><tr><td>T1036.005</td><td>Masquerading: Match Legitimate Name or Location</td><td><code>OneDrive.exe</code> and <code>Secur32.dll</code> are dropped to a subdirectory of <code>%LOCALAPPDATA%\Microsoft\OneDrive</code>, which is used by the legitimate OneDrive application.</td></tr><tr><td>T1027.001</td><td>Obfuscated Files or Information: Binary Padding </td><td><code>IVIEWERS.dll</code> is padded with 10 MB of null bytes to inflate the file size. This tactic can be used to evade security products that have a file size limit for analysis.</td></tr><tr><td>T1027.007</td><td>Obfuscated Files or Information: Dynamic API Resolution</td><td>Multiple stages use ROR13 API hashing based on the uppercase names of the APIs.</td></tr><tr><td>T1027.009</td><td>Obfuscated Files or Information: Embedded Payloads</td><td>The next stage payloads are XOR encoded in the resources of stages 1 to 3.</td></tr><tr><td>T1082</td><td>System Information Discovery </td><td>MadMxShell’s C2 command types 4 and 5 were utilized to enumerate system information and transmit data to the attacker’s C2 server.</td></tr><tr><td>T1083</td><td>File and Directory Discovery</td><td>MadMxShell’s C2 command type 6 was utilized to enumerate files and directories on the infected machine.</td></tr><tr><td>T1033</td><td>System Owner/User Discovery</td><td>MadMxShell sends the current username as part of the information collected by command type 4.</td></tr><tr><td>T1005</td><td>Data from Local System</td><td>MadMxShell can read files on the infected machine when the C2 command type 6 is issued.</td></tr><tr><td>T1071.004</td><td>Application Layer Protocol: DNS</td><td>MadMxShell abuses the DNS MX queries to establish C2 communication with the C2 server.</td></tr><tr><td>T1132.002</td><td>Data Encoding: Non-Standard Encoding</td><td>C2 traffic uses custom encoding based on a lookup table.</td></tr><tr><td>T1572</td><td>Protocol Tunneling</td><td>The C2 protocol encodes data within MX queries and responses of the DNS protocol.</td></tr><tr><td>T1041</td><td>Exfiltration Over C2 Channel</td><td>The collected data is exfiltrated via C2 communications.</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="appendix"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2><br/>Appendix</h2><p>Visit our <a href="https://github.com/threatlabz/tools/blob/main/madmxshell/network_decoder.py">GitHub repository</a> to access the Python script to decode C2 traffic.</p>

in March of 2024, Zscaler ThreatLabz observed a threat actor weaponizing a cluster of domains masquerading as legitimate IP scanner software sites to distribute a previously unseen backdoor. The threat actor registered multiple look-alike domains using a typosquatting technique and leveraged Google Ads to push these domains to the top of search engine results targeting specific search keywords, thereby luring victims to visit these sites.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe newly discovered backdoor uses several techniques such as multiple stages of DLL sideloading, abusing the DNS protocol for communicating with the command-and-control (C2) server, and evading memory forensics security solutions. We named this backdoor “MadMxShell” for its use of DNS MX queries for C2 communication and its very short interval between C2 requests.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn this blog, we will examine the campaign details, threat actor's infrastructure, and a detailed technical analysis of the backdoor. This aligns with the recent trend observed where advanced persistent threat (APT) groups, such as \u003ca href=\"https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2021/10/25/nobelium-targeting-delegated-administrative-privileges-to-facilitate-broader-attacks/\"\u003eNOBELIUM\u003c/a\u003e, crafted attacks targeting these teams. With their privileged access to internal systems and networks, IT security and network management teams are attractive targets for both APT groups and initial access brokers (IABs) that sell access to compromised networks. Although we have not yet attributed the attack described in this blog to a specific threat actor, it is important to highlight this emerging trend.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThreat actors have previously leveraged Google malvertising to distribute trojanized versions of a specific port scanning tool called Advanced IP Scanner, as described in reports by \u003ca href=\"https://securelist.com/uncommon-infection-and-malware-propagation-methods/107640/\"\u003eKaspersky\u003c/a\u003e, \u003ca href=\"https://blogs.blackberry.com/en/2022/10/unattributed-romcom-threat-actor-spoofing-popular-apps-now-hits-ukrainian-militaries\"\u003eBlackBerry\u003c/a\u003e, and \u003ca href=\"https://www.huntress.com/blog/analyzing-a-malicious-advanced-ip-scanner-google-ad-redirection\"\u003eHuntress\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlthough the campaign discussed in this blog uses a similar distribution method, the range of spoofed software has been expanded beyond Advanced IP Scanner. Furthermore, to the best of our knowledge, the final malware delivered in this campaign has not been publicly documented before. Due to these significant differences, we assess with a high-confidence level that this campaign was conducted by a different threat actor.\u003c/p\u003e\n"},"fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":null}]},{"__typename":"ParagraphBlogContainer","fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":"Attack Chain","fieldReusableParagraphs":[{"__typename":"ParagraphWysiwygModule","fieldWysiwygModContent":{"__typename":"Text","value":"\u003ch2\u003eAttack Chain\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe figure below illustrates the multi-stage attack chain at a high level.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/attack_chain.png\" alt=\"Figure 1: The MadMxShell end-to-end attack-chain, which starts with malvertising, followed by multiple intermediate stages of DLL sideloading, and finally DNS tunneling to the C2 server.\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003eFigure 1: The MadMxShell end-to-end attack-chain, which starts with malvertising, followed by multiple intermediate stages of DLL sideloading, and finally DNS tunneling to the C2 server.\u003c/p\u003e\n"},"fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":null}]},{"__typename":"ParagraphBlogContainer","fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":"Technical Analysis","fieldReusableParagraphs":[{"__typename":"ParagraphWysiwygModule","fieldWysiwygModContent":{"__typename":"Text","value":"\u003ch2\u003eTechnical Analysis\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the following section, we provide a detailed analysis for each stage of the attack chain.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eGoogle malvertising campaign\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe modus operandi of the threat actor includes registering multiple look-alike domains spoofing popular port scanning software and pushing them to the top of Google search results by running Google Ads campaigns. This technique is widely known as \u003cstrong\u003emalvertising\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDuring our investigation, we observed users being served these ads when they searched for keywords related to any of the following:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eAny of the legitimate port scanning and IT management software spoofed by this threat actor\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eAdvanced IP Scanner\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAngry IP Scanner\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003ePRTG IP Scanner by Paessler\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eManage Engine\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eNetwork admin tasks related to virtual local area networks (VLANs)\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eScanning IP protocol\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe figure below shows details of the Google Ads campaign carried out by the threat actor in March 2024 for one of the malicious domains. The domain in question was advanced-ip-scanz[.]net, and the search keywords were:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\"advanced ip scanner\"\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\"ip address scanner\"\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cimg src=\"/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/google_ad_campaign_details.png\" alt=\"Figure 2: Details of the Google Ad campaign in March 2024 for the malicious domain advanced-ip-scanz[.]net.\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003eFigure 2: Details of the Google Ads campaign in March 2024 for the malicious domain advanced-ip-scanz[.]net.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOnce the user clicks on any of the attacker-controlled Google Ads, they are redirected to a look-alike site for the corresponding IP scanning software.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eMalicious sites\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe threat actor registered multiple sites masquerading as legitimate IP and port scanner software programs. One such site we observed is advansed-ip-scanner[.]net, which is a look-alike site of the legitimate Advanced IP scanner software www.advanced-ip-scanner[.]com.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe complete source code of the fraudulent website mirrors the legitimate site, with the exception of minor edits made by the threat actor to JavaScript (JS) code which redirects the user to download a malicious file when they click the download button.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe figure below shows a comparison between the altered JS code from the malicious file and the original legitimate website. The \u003ccode\u003ecreateFunctionWithTimeout\u003c/code\u003e function was modified to redirect users to download a malicious ZIP archive file from the following URL: \u003cbr\u003eadvansed-ip-scanner[.]net/yftyudruo.php.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/js_code_comparison.png\" alt=\"Figure 3: JavaScript code comparison between the legitimate website’s createFunctionWithTimeout function with the malicious website.\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cbr\u003eFigure 3: JavaScript code comparison between the legitimate website’s \u003ccode\u003ecreateFunctionWithTimeout\u003c/code\u003e function and the malicious website's code.\u003c/p\u003e\n"},"fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":null}]},{"__typename":"ParagraphBlogContainer","fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":"Backdoor Details - Binary Analysis","fieldReusableParagraphs":[{"__typename":"ParagraphWysiwygModule","fieldWysiwygModContent":{"__typename":"Text","value":"\u003ch2\u003eBackdoor Details - Binary Analysis\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ch3\u003eStage 1 injector\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe analysis in this blog is based on this ZIP archive: \u003ccode\u003eAdvanced-ip-scanner.zip (SHA256:7966ee1ae9042e7345a55aa98ddeb4f39133216438d67461c7ee39864292e015)\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe ZIP archive contains two files:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003eAdvanced-ip-scanner.exe\u003c/code\u003e: A renamed copy of the legitimate Microsoft EXE \u003ccode\u003eoleview.exe\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003eIVIEWERS.dll\u003c/code\u003e: A 22 MB DLL, which contains the stage two payload. This DLL is padded with an unused overlay of 10 MB which prevents scanning by security products that limit the size of analyzed files.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen \u003ccode\u003eAdvanced-ip-scanner.exe\u003c/code\u003e is run, it sideloads \u003ccode\u003eIVIEWERS.dll\u003c/code\u003e which executes a series of heavily obfuscated shellcodes extracted from various locations within the \u003ccode\u003e.rsrc\u003c/code\u003e section of the DLL. The final shellcode extracts and decodes an executable file with the XOR key \u003ccode\u003e5dsadas435235bgdsgdfbvb3253453425345gfdsgfdgdf\u003c/code\u003e from resource \u003ccode\u003eAT21\u003c/code\u003e of the DLL and injects it into a new \u003ccode\u003eAdvanced-ip-scanner.exe\u003c/code\u003e process via process hollowing.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eStage 2 dropper\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe injected EXE file contains the next stage payloads in resource ID \u003ccode\u003e202\u003c/code\u003e, encoded with a hardcoded 8-byte XOR key \u003ccode\u003eF2 09 CD 2D 85 CD 1D A3\u003c/code\u003e and compressed with zlib. Each encoded byte in this resource is padded with seven null bytes, resulting in a 10MB file, likely as another anti-scanning technique. This is shown in the figure below.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/stage_3_payload.png\" alt=\"Figure 4: The encoded and compressed stage 3 payload in the resource.\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003eFigure 4: The encoded and compressed stage 3 payload in the resource.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAfter decoding and decompressing the resource, two files, \u003ccode\u003eOneDrive.exe\u003c/code\u003e and \u003ccode\u003eSecur32.dll\u003c/code\u003e, are dropped into \u003ccode\u003e%LOCALAPPDATA%\\Microsoft\\OneDrive\\Update\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe dropper deletes the stage 1 EXE with the following command before executing the dropped \u003ccode\u003eOneDrive.exe\u003c/code\u003e with \u003ccode\u003eShellExecuteExW\u003c/code\u003e:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"language-shell\"\u003ecmd.exe /C for /l %x in (0,0,0) do (ping -n 3 127.0.0.1 \u0026gt; NUL \u0026amp; for %p in (\"\u0026lt;PATH\u0026gt;\\Advanced-ip-scanner.exe\") do (del /f /q %p \u0026amp; if not exist %p exit))\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003ch3\u003e\u003cbr\u003eStage 3 launcher\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ccode\u003eOneDrive.exe\u003c/code\u003e, a legitimate signed Microsoft EXE, is abused to sideload \u003ccode\u003eSecur32.dll\u003c/code\u003e which sets up persistence for \u003ccode\u003eOneDrive.exe\u003c/code\u003e before executing the embedded stage 4 shellcode.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eData from \u003ccode\u003eSecur32.dll\u003c/code\u003e’s icon resource ID \u003ccode\u003e202\u003c/code\u003e is XOR decoded to obtain the stage 4 shellcode. This is shown in the figure below.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/stage_4_payload.png\" alt=\"Figure 5: The icon resource with the encoded stage 4 payload.\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003eFigure 5: The icon resource with the encoded stage 4 payload.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first 16 bytes of the resource contain an encoded key for the payload that follows it. Each of the first 8 lowercase characters (onedrive) of the current process filename is added to every second byte of the encoded key to derive the XOR key \u003ccode\u003eF2 78 CD 9B 85 32 1D 07 33 C4 A0 21 98 A2 95 E3\u003c/code\u003e, as shown in the figure below. This prevents the correct decoding of the next stage payload if \u003ccode\u003eSecur32.dll\u003c/code\u003e was not sideloaded by \u003ccode\u003eOneDrive.exe\u003c/code\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/generating_xor_key.png\" alt=\"Figure 6: Generating the XOR key to decode the stage 3 shellcode.\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003eFigure 6: Generating the XOR key to decode the stage 3 shellcode.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe malware then attempts to disable Windows Defender by setting \u003ccode\u003eHKLM\\SOFTWARE\\Policies\\Microsoft\\Windows Defender\\DisableAntiSpyware\u003c/code\u003e to \u003ccode\u003e1\u003c/code\u003e in the registry.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt configures a scheduled task named “OneDrive Update” that executes \u003ccode\u003e%LOCALAPPDATA%\\Microsoft\\OneDrive\\Update\\OneDrive.exe\u003c/code\u003e when the current user logs on to Windows before redirecting to the next stage.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eStage 4 backdoor\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe shellcode is a backdoor that allows the threat actor to collect system information, execute commands via \u003ccode\u003ecmd.exe\u003c/code\u003e, and perform basic file manipulation operations such as reading, writing, and deleting files.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo deter analysis and detection, the malware decodes the code of each function with an 8-byte XOR key \u003ccode\u003eF2 09 CD 2D 85 CD 1D A3\u003c/code\u003e (same key used in stage 2), calls the function, and then immediately re-encodes the code. In the figure below, the code excerpt on the right is decoded from the original bytes on the left. We can observe that even after decoding, it still needs to perform an additional step of decoding the \u003ccode\u003eget_c2_domain\u003c/code\u003e function before it can call it. The function code is re-encoded back to its original state before execution continues. This ensures that there is never a fully decoded copy of the shellcode in memory at any point of execution. Most sensitive strings and data, such as the C2 domain, lookup table for encoding/decoding C2 communications, and aforementioned XOR key, are also stored as stack strings to hinder analysis.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/function_bytes.png\" alt=\"Figure 7: Function bytes before and after decoding. Note decoded function includes calls to decode and encode other functions called within it.  \" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003eFigure 7: Function bytes before and after decoding. Note that the decoded function includes calls to decode and encode other functions called within it.  \u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe malware generates a 4-byte session ID with the \u003ccode\u003eCryptGenRandom\u003c/code\u003e API and a victim ID by concatenating the hard disk serial number, computer name, and username, and taking the first 8 bytes of its MD5 hash.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch4\u003eC2 Protocol\u003c/h4\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe malware communicates with the C2 server, litterbolo[.]com, by sending requests and receiving commands encoded within DNS MX The malware then repeats the entire process by sending a heartbeat request (type 0) to retrieve the next command. \u003c/p\u003e\u003ch4\u003eData Encoding \u003c/h4\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe malware sends requests to the C2 server by encoding the data in the subdomain(s) of the Fully Qualified Domain Name (FQDN) in a DNS MX query packet. The C2 responds similarly by encoding its commands as subdomain(s) in the corresponding DNS MX response packet.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eEach byte of binary data is converted into a pair of alphanumeric characters using a custom encoding scheme involving a hardcoded 36-character lookup table. Blocks of 60 alphanumeric characters are separated by a “.” character to represent a subdomain name. Python code for decoding these subdomains into the original request and C2 messages can be found in our \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/threatlabz/tools/blob/main/madmxshell/network_decoder.py\"\u003eGitHub repository\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBecause the malware uses a maximum of 224 characters for the FQDN and the C2 domain name cannot be used to encode data, each DNS packet can only transfer up to 103 bytes. Requests and commands that exceed this size are split into multiple DNS packets and are sent sequentially after the other party has acknowledged receipt of the previous packet.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003ePossibly due to the limited bandwidth of the C2 protocol, this malware is configured with relatively short intervals (3 seconds) between requests. Because of this, its C2 traffic is significantly more noisy than the typical malware utilizing HTTP for C2 communication.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eCommands received from the C2 (after decoding) are structured as shown in the table below.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\u003ctbody\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eOffset\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLength\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eName\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDescription\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003e0x0\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003e4\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eQuery number\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eStarts from \u003ccode\u003e0\u003c/code\u003e per session.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003e0x4\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003e4\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eChecksum\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eAdler-32 checksum of entire message data.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003e0x8\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003e4\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003ePacket number\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eA single message may be split into multiple packets. The structure of subsequent bytes differs slightly for each command type.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003c/tbody\u003e\u003c/table\u003e\u003cp\u003eTable 3: The C2 message structure.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor example, the C2 server for this sample always responds with \u003ccode\u003e33qqooggxr77mdxx88jj6600ev44yyzz9bee99wwuu.litterbolo.com\u003c/code\u003e upon receiving a registration request. This is decoded as \u003ccode\u003e00 00 00 00 03 00 0f 00 00 00 00 00 05 00 00 00 02 00 00 00 00\u003c/code\u003e and represents the following message:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eQuery This aligns with the Google Ads abuse technique used by the threat actor to launch their own malvertising campaign.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe figures below show two posts made by the threat actor on blackhatworld[.]com expressing interest in this technique and asking to enroll in the course.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cimg src=\"/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/----category-images/data-breach/blackhat_website_posts.png\" alt=\"Figure 9: Posts made by the threat actor showing interest in the Google Ad abuse course.\" loading=\"lazy\" decoding=\"async\"\u003e\u003cp\u003eFigure 9: Posts made by the threat actor showing interest in the Google Ads abuse course.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eGoogle Ads threshold accounts and techniques for abusing them are often traded on BlackHat forums. Many times they offer a way for the threat actor to add as many credits as possible to run Google Ads campaigns. This allows the threat actors to run campaigns without actually paying until the threshold limit. A reasonably high threshold limit lets the threat actor run the ad campaign for a significant amount of time. Once the threshold has reached, they can use the same technique with a new Google Ads account to repeat the process.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThreat actors often use virtual credit cards (VCC) along with residential proxies to verify these Google Ads accounts and employ various methods to use them up to the maximum threshold.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis approach effectively enables threat actors to run long lasting malvertising campaigns with a low investment and also avoid account suspension.\u003c/p\u003e\n"},"fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":null}]},{"__typename":"ParagraphBlogContainer","fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":"Conclusion","fieldReusableParagraphs":[{"__typename":"ParagraphWysiwygModule","fieldWysiwygModContent":{"__typename":"Text","value":"\u003ch2\u003eConclusion\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe threat discussed in this blog demonstrates advanced tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs), displaying a keen interest in targeting users in the IT security and network administration teams. The threat actor put significant effort into remaining undetected by evading memory forensics and network security controls.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile we cannot currently attribute this activity to any known threat actor, we continue to monitor any new developments associated with this threat actor and ensure the necessary protections are in place for our customers against these threats.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe also suggest users follow security best practices and exercise caution when clicking on links appearing in Google search engine results. payload.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1055.012\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eProcess Injection: Process Hollowing\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eThe threat actor employs process hollowing to inject and execute the stage 2 dropper EXE file.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1562.001\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eImpair Defenses: Disable or Modify Tools\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eThe stage 3 launcher attempts to disable Windows Defender.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1070.004\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eIndicator Removal: File Deletion\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eThe stage 2 dropper runs a command via \u003ccode\u003ecmd.exe\u003c/code\u003e to delete the stage 1 EXE from disk.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1053.005\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eScheduled Task/Job: Scheduled Task \u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eThe stage 3 launcher configures for persistence by masquerading as a scheduled task. \u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1036.004\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eMasquerading: Masquerade Task or Service\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eThe scheduled task masquerades as a OneDrive update to execute \u003ccode\u003eOneDrive.exe\u003c/code\u003e for malware persistence.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1036.005\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eMasquerading: Match Legitimate Name or Location\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003e\u003ccode\u003eOneDrive.exe\u003c/code\u003e and \u003ccode\u003eSecur32.dll\u003c/code\u003e are dropped to a subdirectory of \u003ccode\u003e%LOCALAPPDATA%\\Microsoft\\OneDrive\u003c/code\u003e, which is used by the legitimate OneDrive application.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1027.001\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eObfuscated Files or Information: Binary Padding \u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003e\u003ccode\u003eIVIEWERS.dll\u003c/code\u003e is padded with 10 MB of null bytes to inflate the file size. This tactic can be used to evade security products that have a file size limit for analysis.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1027.007\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eObfuscated Files or Information: Dynamic API Resolution\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eMultiple stages use ROR13 API hashing based on the uppercase names of the APIs.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1027.009\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eObfuscated Files or Information: Embedded Payloads\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eThe next stage payloads are XOR encoded in the resources of stages 1 to 3.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1082\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eSystem Information Discovery \u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eMadMxShell’s C2 command types 4 and 5 were utilized to enumerate system information and transmit data to the attacker’s C2 server.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1083\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eFile and Directory Discovery\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eMadMxShell’s C2 command type 6 was utilized to enumerate files and directories on the infected machine.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1033\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eSystem Owner/User Discovery\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eMadMxShell sends the current username as part of the information collected by command type 4.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1005\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eData from Local System\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eMadMxShell can read files on the infected machine when the C2 command type 6 is issued.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1071.004\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eApplication Layer Protocol: DNS\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eMadMxShell abuses the DNS MX queries to establish C2 communication with the C2 server.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1132.002\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eData Encoding: Non-Standard Encoding\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eC2 traffic uses custom encoding based on a lookup table.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1572\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eProtocol Tunneling\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eThe C2 protocol encodes data within MX queries and responses of the DNS protocol.\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eT1041\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eExfiltration Over C2 Channel\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eThe collected data is exfiltrated via C2
